how do u build the front end

To build the PureTask front end, you’re basically building the screens + navigation + state that sit on top of your backend (credits/jobs/payments) and your automations (n8n).

Here’s the clean, real-world way to do it.

1) Pick your front-end “container”
You have 3 common routes:

Web app only (fastest): Next.js (React)

Mobile app (best UX): React Native (Expo)

Both from one codebase: React Native + Expo + “web build”

If you want PureTask to feel like Uber, I’d do React Native (Expo) so you get iOS + Android from one build.

2) Build the core screens (MVP)
Don’t start with pixels. Start with routes + flows.

Client (customer) screens
Auth (Sign up / login)

Buy credits (wallet top-up)

Book a cleaning (select cleaner → type → hours → hold credits)

Job status (created → accepted → in progress)

Approval screen (approve / dispute)

Receipt / history

Cleaner screens
Auth + onboarding

Profile + availability

Set rates (tier-based sliders)

Job feed + accept

Job in progress (GPS check-in/out + photos)

Earnings wallet + payout setup

Admin (optional but huge)
Disputes queue

Refund tools

Cleaner verification + tier overrides

KPI dashboard

3) Set up navigation + state
Use:

Stack navigation (Auth stack vs App stack)

Global state for session + user + wallet balances
(Zustand or Redux Toolkit)

Rule: the UI should always be driven by server truth:

job status

held credits

wallet balance

payout eligibility

4) Hook the UI to your backend + n8n
You’ll need:

API client (fetch/axios)

Auth tokens stored securely

Polling or realtime updates (simple polling works fine at MVP)

Typical pattern:

Frontend calls your API: POST /jobs

Backend writes job row + emits event (or triggers n8n)

n8n runs automations (notifications, ledger updates, etc.)

Frontend fetches updated job + wallet state

5) Build reusable UI components (so you don’t drown)
Make these once:

CleanerCard

CreditWalletCard

PrimaryButton

RateSlider

JobStatusBanner

PhotoUploader

Map/GPS check-in button

This is where speed comes from.

6) Handle the “hard” front-end parts early
These are the usual PureTask blockers:

GPS check-in/out (location permission + anti-fake rules later)

Before/after photo uploads (camera + storage + upload progress)

Push notifications (OneSignal)

Payments UI (Stripe client side, but your backend controls intent creation)

7) QA checklist (front-end specific)
Before launch, test these end-to-end:

Buy credits → wallet updates

Book → credits held

Cleaner accepts → client notified

Check-in → job in progress

Upload photos → visible to client

Approve → final charge + refund leftovers

Cleaner earnings → weekly payout shown

If you tell me what you’re using for the backend (Base44? custom Node? Supabase? Firebase?) and whether you want web or mobile first, I’ll lay out the exact folder structure + screens list + API endpoints the front end needs so your dev work becomes plug-and-play.








wat is cloudflare?

Cloudflare is a security + performance service that sits in front of your website or app.

Think of it as a shield + accelerator between users and your servers.

Simple explanation (plain English)
When someone visits your app or website:

User → Cloudflare → Your server

Cloudflare:

protects you from attacks

makes your site load faster

keeps your site online if something goes wrong

What Cloudflare actually does (the important parts)
1. Security (huge)
Cloudflare protects your app from:

hackers

bots

fake traffic

DDoS attacks (when someone tries to crash your site with traffic)

For PureTask, this is critical because you have:

logins

payments

Stripe webhooks

admin dashboards

Cloudflare blocks bad traffic before it ever reaches n8n, your API, or your database.

2. Speed (CDN)
Cloudflare has servers all over the world.

It:

caches images, scripts, and pages

serves them from the closest location to the user

Result:

faster load times

smoother UX

better SEO

Example:
A user in New York doesn’t have to wait for your West Coast server.

3. DNS (domain control)
Cloudflare usually becomes your DNS provider.

That means:

you manage puretask.com

you point it to:

your frontend

your API

n8n

Stripe webhooks

Cloudflare = control center for where traffic goes.

4. Free HTTPS / SSL
Cloudflare gives you:

HTTPS

SSL certificates

automatic renewal

No setup pain.

This is required for:

Stripe

logins

mobile apps

browser trust

5. Firewall rules
You can say things like:

“Block traffic from these countries”

“Only allow Stripe webhooks to hit this endpoint”

“Rate-limit login attempts”

For PureTask, this is 🔥 because you can:

lock down /stripe/webhook

protect /admin

stop brute-force attacks

What Cloudflare is NOT
❌ It is not your backend
❌ It does not store your database
❌ It does not replace n8n, Base44, or Stripe

It protects and optimizes them.

How Cloudflare fits into PureTask (specifically)
For PureTask, Cloudflare should sit in front of:

app.puretask.com (frontend)

api.puretask.com (backend)

n8n.puretask.com (automations + webhooks)

Flow example:
Client books a cleaning →
Stripe sends webhook →
Cloudflare checks it →
Passes it to n8n →
n8n updates database + payouts

If Cloudflare sees something sketchy → blocked.

Do you need Cloudflare?
Yes. 100%.

Any marketplace with:

payments

contractors

wallets

automation endpoints

…should be using Cloudflare.

It’s:

free to start

industry standard

used by Stripe, Shopify, Discord, Uber-scale apps

TL;DR
Cloudflare is:

🛡️ security

⚡ speed

🌍 global delivery

🔒 HTTPS

🧠 traffic control

It’s the first line of defense for PureTask.

Next logical step (when you’re ready)
I can:

tell you exactly how to set Cloudflare up for PureTask

define which routes should be protected

design firewall rules for Stripe + n8n

map your domain structure cleanly

Just say:
“Set up Cloudflare for PureTask.”


is there software that takes the backend data and creates the front end?

Yes — there is software that turns backend data into a frontend, but it’s important to understand the different levels, because they’re not all equal.

I’ll explain this clearly, then tell you what actually makes sense for PureTask (not theory).

Short answer (plain English)
There is no magic tool that perfectly builds a custom consumer app UI from raw backend logic.

But there ARE tools that:

auto-generate admin dashboards

auto-render CRUD screens

bind databases/APIs to UI components

dramatically reduce frontend work

Most real companies use a hybrid approach.

The 4 categories (from weakest → strongest)
1️⃣ Admin / Internal Tools (YES, very real)
These tools read your backend data and instantly generate a frontend.

They are GREAT for:

admin panels

ops dashboards

internal tools

back-office views

Examples
Retool

Supabase Studio

Hasura Console

Appsmith

Forest Admin

What they do
Connect to your database or API

Auto-generate tables, forms, filters

Let you edit records visually

What they do NOT do
❌ Not good for consumer-facing apps
❌ Not good for polished UX
❌ Not mobile-app friendly

Verdict
✅ You should absolutely use this for:

admin panel

cleaner moderation

payouts review

disputes

metrics

2️⃣ Low-Code / No-Code App Builders (PARTIAL)
These tools connect to backend data and let you visually build screens.

Examples
Bubble

FlutterFlow

WeWeb

Framer + CMS

Softr

What they do
Bind backend data to UI components

Generate real frontends

Handle auth, forms, lists, detail views

Limits
❌ Hard to scale long-term
❌ Complex logic gets messy
❌ Performance limits
❌ Vendor lock-in

Verdict
⚠️ Good for MVPs
⚠️ Not ideal for a serious marketplace long-term

3️⃣ Headless Backend + UI Bindings (BEST PRACTICE)
This is what real production apps use.

Your backend exposes:

structured APIs

schemas

events

Your frontend:

consumes those APIs

renders UI intentionally

But tooling helps bridge the gap.

Examples
Supabase + React

Firebase + Flutter

PostgREST

tRPC

GraphQL

Stripe Elements (auto-UI from backend config)

This gives you:

backend-driven logic

frontend-controlled UX

Verdict
✅ This is what PureTask should do

4️⃣ True “Backend → UI Auto-Generation” (REALITY CHECK)
People want:

“I define logic and data, and the app UI appears.”

That only works well for:

internal tools

CRUD apps

enterprise dashboards

It does not work well for:

consumer UX

marketplaces

apps with trust, emotion, polish

Uber, Airbnb, Stripe, DoorDash:

do NOT auto-generate their frontends

they use backend-driven rules + handcrafted UI

What PureTask should ACTUALLY do (clear recommendation)
✅ Use auto-generated UI for:
Admin dashboard

Cleaner management

Job review

Payout approval

Metrics & KPIs

(Totally fine to be ugly — users never see it.)

✅ Use hand-crafted frontend for:
Client booking flow

Cleaner onboarding

Wallets

Job progress

Approval / dispute UX

This is where trust lives.

The smartest PureTask stack (realistic + scalable)
Backend (you already have this)
PostgreSQL

Stripe

n8n

Event-driven logic

Admin UI (auto)
Retool / Appsmith / Supabase Studio

App UI (intentional)
React / Next.js or Flutter

Backend-driven rules (rates, tiers, states)

Bonus
Schema → form generators for internal flows

Backend config controls UI behavior (not layout)

Key insight (this matters)
Backend defines the rules.
Frontend defines the experience.

Trying to auto-generate experience is how apps feel cheap.

PureTask’s value:

trust

clarity

“approve before paying”

proof (photos, GPS)

That must be designed, not generated.

If you want, next I can:
map exactly which PureTask screens can be auto-generated

design an admin UI that takes 1 day, not 3 weeks

define a backend schema that drives frontend behavior cleanly

show how Stripe does “backend → UI” the right way

Just say:
“Map backend → frontend for PureTask.”


so wat make it pretty?

Great question — this is the right question.

Short answer first, then the real breakdown:

What makes it “pretty” is NOT the backend and NOT the data.
What makes it pretty is intentional design + frontend decisions.

Now let’s unpack that clearly 👇

The honest truth (no fluff)
Backend data can tell the app:

what to show

when to show it

what state it’s in

But it cannot decide how it should feel.

“Pretty” comes from 5 frontend pillars.

1️⃣ Visual Design System (this is #1)
This is the biggest factor.

A design system defines:

colors

spacing

fonts

shapes

motion

hierarchy

If this is weak → the app looks cheap
If this is strong → the app feels premium

For PureTask, this includes:

your agreed brand colors

rounded pill buttons

consistent spacing

friendly but confident typography

calm, trustworthy contrast

👉 This is created in Figma, not in the backend.

2️⃣ Layout & Spacing (invisible, but huge)
Pretty apps are mostly empty space.

Bad apps:

cram everything

tight spacing

no breathing room

Good apps:

big margins

clear sections

one main action per screen

Example:

Booking screen = ONE primary CTA

Job screen = ONE status at a time

Wallet = ONE balance focus

Spacing is a frontend decision — auto-generated UIs fail here.

3️⃣ Typography (more important than colors)
Fonts control trust.

Why Stripe, Uber, Airbnb feel legit:

consistent font scale

calm line height

predictable text hierarchy

Pretty apps:

use 2 fonts max

clear size ladder (H1 → H2 → body → microcopy)

readable contrast

Ugly apps:

random font sizes

inconsistent weights

bad line spacing

Again: frontend + design system.

4️⃣ Micro-interactions & Motion (the “alive” factor)
This is subtle but powerful.

Pretty apps:

buttons gently scale on tap

loaders feel intentional

transitions are smooth

states change clearly

Examples:

“Cleaner on the way” slides in

Approval button pulses slightly

Credits update with a soft animation

Auto-generated frontends rarely do this well.

5️⃣ UX Decisions (clarity > features)
Pretty ≠ flashy
Pretty = clear

Great UX:

one decision per screen

obvious next step

no cognitive overload

This is why:

Stripe Checkout feels amazing

Uber is simple

Airbnb flows feel calm

They hide complexity behind clean UI.

Why “backend → frontend generators” look ugly
Because they:

optimize for data completeness

not emotional clarity

not trust

not brand

They answer:

“What fields exist?”

Not:

“What should the user feel right now?”

The mental model you should lock in
Backend = brain
Frontend = face

You don’t auto-generate a face and expect it to be attractive.

What makes PureTask pretty specifically
For PureTask, “pretty” means:

calm (trust + payments)

clean (cleaning brand)

friendly (contractors + clients)

confident (approve-before-pay)

That comes from:

intentional color usage (not too many)

big clear CTAs

friendly rounded shapes

visual proof (photos, GPS, status)

minimal text, strong hierarchy

So… who/what actually makes it pretty?
Tools

how can i download my project from github

Here are the 4 correct ways to download a project from GitHub, from easiest → most “developer-correct”. Pick the one that matches your comfort level.

✅ Method 1: Download ZIP (fastest, no tools)
Best if you just want the files on your computer.

Go to your GitHub repository in the browser

Click the green “Code” button

Click “Download ZIP”

Unzip the file on your computer

That’s it.

⚠️ Notes:

This does not include git history

You can’t easily push changes back unless you re-init git

✅ Method 2: Clone with Git (best practice)
Best if you plan to edit, version, and push changes back.

Step 1: Copy repo URL
Click Code

Copy the HTTPS URL
(example: https://github.com/username/project.git)

Step 2: Open terminal (or Command Prompt)
Run:

git clone https://github.com/username/project.git
This creates a local folder with:

full project

full commit history

ability to push/pull updates

📌 You’ll need Git installed
Check with:

git --version
✅ Method 3: GitHub Desktop (no terminal)
Best if you don’t want to use command line.

Download GitHub Desktop

Sign in to your GitHub account

Go to your repo in the browser

Click Code → Open with GitHub Desktop

Choose a local folder

Click Clone

Now you have:

local project

visual UI for commits & pushes

✅ Method 4: Download a specific branch or folder
Download a specific branch
Switch branches in GitHub UI

Click Code → Download ZIP

Download only one folder (advanced)
GitHub doesn’t support this natively, but tools exist:

git sparse-checkout

third-party downloaders

(Only worth it for large repos.)

Which should you use?
For PureTask, I recommend:

If you’re:
reviewing code

moving it to another environment

backing it up

➡ Method 1 (ZIP)

If you’re:
actively building

collaborating

deploying

➡ Method 2 (git clone) or Method 3 (GitHub Desktop)

Common mistakes to avoid
❌ Downloading ZIP and thinking it’s “connected” to GitHub
❌ Editing files without git if you plan to deploy
❌ Losing environment variables (they are NOT in the repo)

Next step I can help with
If you want, I can:

walk you through cloning your exact repo

explain folder structure

prep it for deployment

connect it to Cloudflare / hosting

clean up repo for production

Just tell me:
“I’m using Windows / Mac” and
“ZIP or Git?”


how can we find out wat screen we should have for the customer interactions

This is a product-design question, not a design-tools question — and that’s good.
There’s a correct way to figure out what screens you need, and it’s not “guessing” or copying other apps.

I’ll walk you through the method, then apply it directly to PureTask customers.

The core principle (lock this in)
Screens are not features.
Screens are answers to user questions at specific moments in time.

So the way we find screens is by asking:

What is the customer trying to do right now?

What uncertainty do they have?

What decision must they make next?

Step 1: Start with the CUSTOMER JOURNEY (not screens)
For PureTask customers, the journey is:

“Can I trust this?”

“How does this work?”

“Who is coming to my home?”

“Is the cleaner actually on the way?”

“Did they do a good job?”

“Am I being charged fairly?”

“What if something went wrong?”

Each of those forces at least one screen.

Step 2: Convert questions → moments → screens
1️⃣ “Can I trust this?”
Moment: First time opening the app

👉 Screen needed:

Landing / Home screen
Purpose:

Explain approve-before-pay

Show trust signals (photos, GPS, verification)

One clear CTA: Book a cleaning

2️⃣ “How does this work?”
Moment: Before committing money

👉 Screen needed:

How it Works screen (or section)
Purpose:

3–4 steps max

Reduce anxiety

Explain credits + approval simply

If you skip this, people hesitate.

3️⃣ “Who is coming to my home?”
Moment: Choosing a cleaner

👉 Screens needed:

Cleaner List screen

Cleaner Profile screen

Purpose:

Face + name

Reliability score

Reviews

Price range

Verification badges

Trust is visual here.

4️⃣ “What am I paying & what’s being held?”
Moment: Booking

👉 Screen needed:

Booking / Checkout screen

Purpose:

Show:

estimated hours

credits held

buffer explanation

Reinforce: You only pay for time worked

This is where most marketplaces lose users.

5️⃣ “Is the cleaner actually on the way?”
Moment: Day of service

👉 Screen needed:

Job In Progress screen

Purpose:

Status timeline:

accepted

on the way

checked in

in progress

GPS indicator

Calm reassurance

This screen kills anxiety.

6️⃣ “Did they do a good job?”
Moment: Job completed

👉 Screen needed:

Job Completion / Review screen

Purpose:

Before & after photos

Time worked

Final charge

Approve / dispute buttons

This is your core differentiator.

7️⃣ “Am I being charged fairly?”
Moment: Approval

👉 Screen needed:

Approval & Payment Summary screen

Purpose:

Final credits charged

Credits refunded (if any)

Clear confirmation

This builds long-term trust.

8️⃣ “What if something went wrong?”
Moment: Problem or doubt

👉 Screens needed:

Dispute screen

Support / Help screen

Purpose:

Show they’re protected

Simple options, not legal text

Even if rarely used, this increases conversion.

Step 3: Group screens into a MINIMAL customer set
For PureTask customers, the minimum complete set is:

Core customer screens (must-have)
Home / Landing

How It Works

Cleaner List

Cleaner Profile

Booking / Checkout

Job In Progress

Job Completed / Review

Approval / Payment Summary

Wallet / Credits

Support / Dispute

That’s it.
Anything beyond this is secondary.

Step 4: Validate screens with the “What would break if we remove this?” test
Ask for each screen:

“If this didn’t exist, would the customer feel confused, unsafe, or blocked?”

If yes → required
If no → optional or merged

Example:

Notifications screen? Optional (can be settings)

Wallet screen? Required (money clarity)

Profile settings? Optional early on

Step 5: Tie screens to backend STATES (this is where you win)
Each screen exists because the backend has a state.

Example:

Job status = in_progress → Job In Progress screen

Job status = awaiting_approval → Approval screen

Credits on hold → Wallet explanation

This is how backend drives screens, but design decides how they look.

The big insight (this matters)
You don’t design screens first.
You design moments of uncertainty → then create screens to resolve them.

That’s why good apps feel calm and bad ones feel stressful.

What I recommend next (very concrete)
Next, we should:

Lock the customer screen list (we basically just did)

Define what question each screen answers

Sketch low-fidelity wireframes (boxes, not pretty yet)

THEN make it pretty

If you want, I can:

turn this into a 1-page customer screen map

define exact data each screen needs

show which screens can be merged or delayed

map screens → backend states → events

Just say:
“Map the PureTask customer screens step by step.”

wat are the benefits to turning it into a figma wireframes

Great question. This is one of those moments where doing one extra step now saves you months of rework later.

I’ll explain this practically, not in “designer fluff”.

Short answer (plain English)
Figma wireframes turn your app from “a pile of screens” into “one clear product.”

They let you:

see the whole experience at once

remove unnecessary screens before coding

make the app feel calm, premium, and intentional

keep Base44 + frontend work aligned

The REAL benefits (for PureTask specifically)
1️⃣ You stop building the wrong things (biggest benefit)
Right now, Base44 lets you build anything fast — which is dangerous.

Wireframes force you to answer:

Do we actually need this screen?

Can this be a tab instead?

Is this step necessary or just backend logic leaking into UI?

For PureTask:

This is how we cut ~40 customer screens down to ~12

Without breaking functionality

👉 Figma prevents overbuilding.

2️⃣ You design the flow, not individual pages
Base44 = page-by-page thinking
Figma = journey thinking

In Figma you can see:

Home → Discover → Book → In Progress → Approve

where anxiety spikes

where trust must be reinforced

This matters because PureTask is:

payments

strangers entering homes

approval-before-pay (trust-heavy)

You cannot feel flow quality by clicking between Base44 pages.

3️⃣ You separate “state” from “screen”
This is huge.

Right now you have:

BookingRequest

BookingRequestSent

PaymentSuccess

etc.

In Figma you realize:

These are not screens — these are states of the same screen.

Wireframes help you:

merge screens into states

simplify navigation

make the app feel smooth instead of jumpy

This is how Stripe and Uber feel clean.

4️⃣ You make it pretty by design, not by accident
Earlier you asked:

“what makes it pretty?”

This is where it happens.

Wireframes let you:

control spacing

decide hierarchy

choose what’s loud vs quiet

define ONE primary action per screen

You don’t worry about:

colors yet

fonts yet

polish yet

Just:

clarity

calm

intent

Pretty apps start as boring wireframes.

5️⃣ You create a single source of truth
Without Figma:

Base44 UI drifts

frontend code drifts

changes are reactive

With Figma:

Figma = product truth

Base44 = implementation

frontend = execution

Everyone (including future devs) knows:

“This is what PureTask is supposed to feel like.”

6️⃣ You move faster AFTER wireframes (counterintuitive but true)
It feels like:

“This is slowing me down.”

In reality:

fewer revisions

fewer rebuilds

fewer “wait… this feels wrong” moments

Most startups that skip wireframes:

rebuild their booking flow 3–4 times

redesign dashboards repeatedly

never quite feel confident

7️⃣ You can test logic without code
With wireframes you can:

walk through a full booking in 2 minutes

notice confusion instantly

explain the app to anyone clearly

Ask yourself:

“Could I show this to a stranger and they’d get it?”

If yes → you’re ready to build.

What Figma wireframes are NOT
❌ Not pretty designs
❌ Not final UI
❌ Not branding work

They are:

boxes

labels

arrows

intent

That’s why they’re powerful.

What I recommend for PureTask (very specific)
Do NOT wireframe everything.
Wireframe ONLY:

Customer flow (Home → Approval)

Cleaner job workspace

Wallet / credits logic

Ignore:

admin dashboards

legal pages

content pages

Output you get after wireframes
Once wireframes exist, you can:

confidently delete Base44 screens

rebuild flows cleanly

style once (not repeatedly)

hand off to frontend devs easily

stay aligned as features grow

The key sentence to remember
Base44 is great at building screens.
Figma is how you decide which screens deserve to exist.

If you want, next I can:
turn your final customer V1 screens into a wireframe map

tell you exactly which Base44 pages to delete or merge

define wireframes that directly map to your backend states

keep it lean (1–2 hours of work, not weeks)

Just say:
“Create the PureTask wireframe plan.”


when u say hand off to frontend devs easily , waat do u mean exactly? wat would they be doing

Great question — this is one of the most misunderstood parts of building software, so I’m glad you asked it now.

I’ll explain exactly what “handoff to frontend devs” means, what artifacts they receive, and what work they actually do—in concrete, non-hand-wavy terms.

Short, blunt answer
When I say “handoff to frontend devs”, I mean:

You give them a Figma file that already answers what to build, so they can focus entirely on how to build it correctly in code.

They are not deciding product behavior.
They are not guessing flows.
They are not inventing UX.

They are implementing.

What frontend devs are responsible for (and NOT responsible for)
❌ What they should NOT be doing
If wireframes are missing, devs end up doing this (bad):

“Do we need a separate screen for this?”

“What happens after this button?”

“Is this a modal or a page?”

“What’s the primary action here?”

“Should this be editable or read-only?”

“What data do I show first?”

That’s product design, not frontend engineering.

This is how apps become messy.

✅ What frontend devs SHOULD be doing
With Figma wireframes, their job becomes:

Translate screens into components

Hook components to backend data

Handle loading / error states

Make it responsive (mobile / desktop)

Add interactions & animations

Ensure performance & accessibility

That’s where they’re strongest.

What exactly you hand them (the artifacts)
1️⃣ Figma Wireframes (core handoff)
This is the main artifact.

A good PureTask wireframe shows:

every screen that exists

what data appears on each screen

what the primary action is

how you move from one screen/state to another

Example (Job Approval screen):

Before photos (grid)

After photos (grid)

Time worked (read-only)

Credits charged

Credits refunded

Buttons:

Approve

Report Issue

The dev doesn’t decide this.
They implement it.

2️⃣ Interaction notes (usually inside Figma)
These answer:

What happens when I click this?

Is this disabled sometimes?

Does this open a modal?

Is this optimistic or confirmed?

Example note:

“Approve button → calls job.approve → shows loading → success state replaces buttons”

This prevents 20 Slack questions.

3️⃣ State definitions (critical)
This is where your backend meets frontend.

You define states like:

job_status = in_progress

job_status = awaiting_approval

job_status = approved

payment_status = held | released

Frontend devs then:

map states → UI

show/hide components

lock actions appropriately

They are not inventing states.

4️⃣ Design tokens (later stage)
Once you move beyond wireframes, you’ll also hand off:

colors

fonts

spacing rules

button styles

But wireframes come first.

What frontend devs actually do day-to-day
Here’s a realistic breakdown.

Example: “Job In Progress” screen
Frontend dev tasks:

Create layout from wireframe

Fetch job data from API

Subscribe to status updates

Render:

timeline

GPS status

messages

Disable actions based on job state

Handle edge cases:

cleaner late

connection lost

Make it feel smooth

They are engineering the experience, not designing it.

Why this matters financially (very important)
Without wireframes:

devs guess

they build wrong

you rework

you pay twice

With wireframes:

devs build once

less back-and-forth

fewer revisions

faster delivery

This is how companies save tens of thousands of dollars.

Why this is extra important for PureTask
PureTask has:

money

trust

approvals

disputes

contractor classification concerns

If frontend devs guess:

you introduce legal risk

you introduce trust leaks

you create UX anxiety

Wireframes lock the product logic before code exists.

The clean mental model
You (product) decide what exists and why.
Frontend devs decide how it works in code.

Wireframes are the contract between those two worlds.

What happens without wireframes (real talk)
Teams end up with:

duplicated screens

inconsistent flows

“temporary” UI that becomes permanent

features that feel bolted on

constant refactors

That’s what you’re actively avoiding right now.

What I’d do next if I were you
For PureTask, I would:

Finalize customer V1 screen list

Create low-fidelity wireframes only

Add state notes

THEN let frontend devs implement

No colors yet.
No branding yet.
Just clarity.

show you exactly what a frontend dev expects to see in Figma

create a PureTask wireframe checklist

map one screen end-to-end as a concrete example

help you decide what NOT to hand off yet

Just say:

Perfect — this is the exact right mindset for a strong solo founder.
You don’t need a team yet. You need clarity artifacts that let future you (or future devs) execute without second-guessing.

I’ll do exactly what you asked, in three tight sections:

What a frontend dev (or future you) expects to see in Figma

A PureTask wireframe checklist you can reuse

One PureTask screen mapped end-to-end (concrete example)

No fluff. No design jargon. Just execution.

1️⃣ What a frontend dev ACTUALLY expects to see in Figma
Think of Figma as a visual contract.

A frontend dev expects answers to these questions without asking you:

A. What screens exist (and which do NOT)
They want to see:

a finite list of screens

not 3 versions of the same thing

not hidden flows in your head

In Figma this looks like:

one page called:
“PureTask – Customer V1 Screens”

each screen laid out left → right in flow order

This alone saves weeks.

B. What is the PRIMARY action on each screen
Every screen must answer:

“What is the one thing the user should do here?”

In Figma:

the primary button is visually obvious

secondary actions are smaller or muted

If a dev can’t tell the primary action in 2 seconds, the screen isn’t ready.

C. What data appears (not how it’s fetched)
Frontend devs need to know:

what data exists

what is read-only

what is editable

They do not need:

SQL

API routes

Base44 logic

In Figma you show:

labels

placeholders

example values

Example:

“Time worked: 3h 20m (read-only)”

That’s enough.

D. What states exist for the SAME screen
This is the most important and most skipped part.

A dev expects to see:

empty state

loading state

success state

error / blocked state

In Figma this can be:

duplicated frames

or notes next to components

Without this, devs guess — and guessing creates bugs.

E. What happens when I click X
Not code. Just intent.

In Figma notes:

“Approve → releases credits → success confirmation”

“Dispute → opens dispute modal”

“Back → returns to dashboard”

This prevents logic drift.

F. What should NOT be possible
Constraints matter.

Examples:

“Approve disabled until photos are uploaded”

“Cannot edit booking once cleaner checked in”

“Credits locked during job”

These guardrails are critical for PureTask.

Summary (what they expect)
A frontend dev expects Figma to answer:

What exists

What matters

What changes

What’s allowed

What’s not

If Figma answers that, coding is straightforward.

2️⃣ PureTask Wireframe Checklist (copy this)
Use this checklist for every screen you wireframe.

🔲 PureTask Wireframe Checklist
Screen identity
⬜ Screen name (exact, final)

⬜ User role (Client / Cleaner / Admin)

⬜ Entry point (where user comes from)

User intent
⬜ Why does this screen exist?

⬜ What uncertainty does it resolve?

⬜ What decision does it enable?

Actions
⬜ ONE primary action

⬜ Secondary actions (if any)

⬜ Destructive actions clearly marked

Data
⬜ What data is shown

⬜ What is editable vs read-only

⬜ Example values included

States
⬜ Loading

⬜ Success

⬜ Blocked / disabled

⬜ Error (if applicable)

Rules & constraints
⬜ What cannot happen here?

⬜ What is locked by backend state?

⬜ What requires confirmation?

Notes
⬜ What happens after the primary action?

⬜ Where does the user go next?

If all boxes are checked → the screen is ready.

3️⃣ End-to-End Example: Job Approval Screen (Client)
This is the most important PureTask screen, so it’s a perfect example.

Screen: JobApproval.jsx
Role
Client

Entry point
Job status = awaiting_approval

User clicks job from dashboard

User’s mental state
“Did they do a good job — and am I being charged fairly?”

This screen must feel:

calm

transparent

empowering

Wireframe layout (structure, not beauty)
Section 1 — Header
Cleaner name + photo

Job date & address

Status badge: “Awaiting your approval”

Section 2 — Proof of Work
Before photos (grid)

After photos (grid)

Read-only
No edits.
No uploads.

Section 3 — Time & Cost Breakdown
Time worked: 3h 20m

Hourly rate: 25 credits/hr

Credits used: 83

Credits refunded: 17

Clear math.
No surprises.

Section 4 — Primary Actions
Primary button

✅ Approve & Release Credits

Secondary

⚠️ Report an Issue

Approve is visually dominant.

States (this is critical)
Default
All data visible

Approve enabled

Loading (after click)
Buttons disabled

Spinner + “Releasing credits…”

Success
Confirmation message

Status changes to “Completed”

Redirect to dashboard after 2s

Dispute state
Modal opens

Select issue type

Optional note

Submit dispute

No immediate charge.

Constraints (rules shown in notes)
Approve disabled if:

No after photos

Dispute available for:

48 hours after completion

No editing any values

Backend mapping (light, not technical)
job.status = awaiting_approval

credits.held → credits.released

cleaner.balance += payout

This helps you later — not the dev.

Why this screen works
One decision

Full transparency

No math anxiety

No hidden logic

Reinforces PureTask’s core promise

This is what “good handoff” looks like.

Final perspective (important for you as a solo founder)
You are not “doing extra work” by wireframing.

You are:

freezing product intent

protecting yourself from rework

making future execution obvious

Figma becomes your external brain.

This prompt is intentionally long, explicit, and opinionated — Lovable performs best when you tell it exactly what the product is, what screens exist, and what NOT to invent.

✅ PURETASK — LOVABLE.DEV MASTER PROMPT
Paste everything below into Lovable.dev as your project prompt

Project Name
PureTask

Product Type
Consumer marketplace web app (and mobile-friendly web)
“Uber-style platform for cleaning services”

High-Level Description
PureTask is a trust-first cleaning marketplace where customers book independent cleaners and only pay after approving the work.

Core trust features:

GPS check-in / check-out

Before & after photos

Credits held in escrow

Client approval before payment release

PureTask is not a cleaning company.
It is a platform that connects clients with independent cleaners.

Tone:

Clean

Calm

Trustworthy

Modern

Friendly but professional

Target Users
Primary: Clients
People booking home cleanings who care about:

trust

transparency

fairness

not being overcharged

Secondary: Cleaners
Independent contractors who:

set their own rates

earn credits

get paid after approval

(Admin users exist but are internal-only.)

Core Mental Model (IMPORTANT)
Screens represent decisions or reassurance moments

States do NOT become new screens

Tabs and modals are preferred over new pages

One primary action per screen

Do NOT invent extra pages.

🔒 LOCKED: Customer V1 Screen List (ONLY THESE)
Lovable should generate ONLY the following customer-facing screens.

1. Home (Public)
Purpose:

Explain what PureTask is

Build trust

Drive booking

Primary CTA:

“Book a Cleaning”

Content:

One-line value prop

3 trust pillars:

GPS tracking

Before & after photos

Approve before paying

2. Auth & Role Selection
Purpose:

Sign in

Choose Client vs Cleaner

Primary action:

Continue with Google (Client)

Notes:

Role is selected once and locked

3. Client Dashboard (Single Hub)
Purpose:

Central control center for clients

Tabs (same screen):

Upcoming Jobs

Past Jobs

Favorites

Recurring (light v1)

Primary CTA:

“Book a Cleaning”

4. Discover Cleaners
Purpose:

Browse and evaluate cleaners

Content:

Cleaner cards with:

photo

name

reliability score

credit rate range

Primary action:

View Cleaner Profile

Notes:

“Smart matching” is a toggle, not a new page

5. Cleaner Profile
Purpose:

Deep evaluation before booking

Content:

Reviews

Reliability score explanation

Rates in credits/hour

Services offered

Primary action:

“Book This Cleaner”

Secondary:

Message cleaner (limited pre-book)

6. Book Cleaning (Step-Based Flow)
Purpose:

Create a booking clearly and safely

Steps (ONE screen, internal steps):

Cleaning type

Hours

Add-ons

Credit hold summary

Critical copy:

“You only pay for time worked”

“Unused credits are refunded automatically”

Primary action:

Confirm Booking

7. Booking Status
Purpose:

Show what happens after booking

States (same screen):

Pending acceptance

Accepted

Declined

Primary action:

View Job

8. Job In Progress
Purpose:

Reassurance during the job

Content:

Status timeline:

Accepted

On the way

Checked in

In progress

GPS indicator

Messaging

Rules:

Credits locked

No edits allowed

9. Job Approval (CORE SCREEN)
Purpose:

Let client approve or dispute work

Content:

Before photos

After photos

Time worked

Credits charged

Credits refunded

Primary action:

“Approve & Release Credits”

Secondary:

“Report an Issue” (modal)

This is the key differentiator of PureTask.

10. Wallet (Credits)
Purpose:

Transparency around money

Content:

Available credits

Held credits

Credit history

Primary action:

Buy Credits

Rules:

Credits are not refundable to cash (client-side)

11. Messages
Purpose:

Communication with cleaners

Rules:

Messages tied to bookings only

No open marketplace chat

12. Help & Legal
Purpose:

Reduce fear and friction

Content:

FAQ

Policies

Support contact

Style:

Reassuring

Plain language

❌ DO NOT CREATE THESE SCREENS
Lovable should NOT generate:

Separate payment success pages

Separate booking history pages

Separate pricing page

Separate review page

Persona landing pages

Standalone dispute pages (use modal)

Visual & UX Direction
Clean spacing

One primary CTA per screen

Rounded buttons

Calm color usage

Minimal clutter

Trust > flash

No dark patterns.
No aggressive upsells.

Backend Awareness (Light)
Lovable does NOT need to implement backend logic, but should respect:

Jobs have states (pending, in progress, awaiting approval, completed)

Credits are held, then released

Cleaner actions drive client status updates

Success Criteria
The app should feel:

calm

transparent

safe to use

easy to explain in under 30 seconds

If a first-time user can understand:

“I book → cleaner works → I approve → I pay”

Then the UI is correct.

Final Instruction to Lovable
Build ONLY what is described above.
Do NOT invent additional screens or flows.
Prefer simplicity, clarity, and trust over feature density.

why do u say its a perfect use case for loveable

Great question — and I’m glad you asked why, not just how.
When I say PureTask is a perfect use case for Lovable, I’m being very specific, not hype-y.

Here’s the clear, grounded reasoning.

The short answer
Lovable is strongest when the product logic is already clear, constrained, and state-driven — which is exactly what you’ve done with PureTask.

You’re not asking Lovable to invent a product.
You’re asking it to express a well-defined product.

That’s the ideal use case.

What Lovable is actually good at (and bad at)
Let’s demystify Lovable first.

Lovable is GOOD at:
Turning clear product intent into UI

Generating coherent flows when screens are limited

Producing clean, modern layouts

Respecting step-based flows

Moving fast when ambiguity is low

Lovable is BAD at:
Inventing product logic

Deciding what screens should exist

Designing trust-sensitive flows from scratch

Handling sprawling, undefined feature sets

Most people fail with Lovable because they do this 👇

“Build me an Uber for X”

You didn’t do that.

Why PureTask fits Lovable unusually well
1️⃣ Your screens are already locked and minimal
Lovable breaks down when:

there are too many screens

responsibilities overlap

states are confused for pages

You’ve already done the hard work:

12 customer screens

clear purpose per screen

states ≠ screens

modals for exceptions

This is exactly what Lovable needs.

2️⃣ PureTask is state-driven, not content-driven
Lovable performs best with apps where:

UI changes based on state

steps are sequential

logic is predictable

PureTask is:

booking → pending → in progress → approval

credits held → released

cleaner actions → client reassurance

This is far easier for Lovable than:

social networks

content platforms

creative tools

Your product is procedural. That’s gold.

3️⃣ You’ve removed the “what should exist?” ambiguity
Lovable struggles most when it has to answer:

“Do we need another page here?”

“Should this be a modal?”

“What happens next?”

Your prompt explicitly says:

ONLY these screens exist

DO NOT invent pages

Prefer tabs and states

One primary action per screen

That turns Lovable from a guesser into an executor.

4️⃣ Trust-first ≠ design-heavy (at this stage)
Apps that fail with Lovable early are ones that need:

emotional branding

heavy visual storytelling

complex visual metaphors

PureTask v1 needs:

clarity

calm

transparency

structure

That’s Lovable’s sweet spot.

You’re not asking it to design a luxury fashion brand.
You’re asking it to design confidence.

5️⃣ You are a solo founder with high product clarity
This matters more than tools.

Lovable works best when:

one person holds the product vision

decisions are consistent

there’s no committee noise

You:

understand your backend

understand your trust model

understand what not to build

That’s why Lovable becomes a force multiplier instead of a liability.

What Lovable gives you specifically
For PureTask, Lovable will:

generate a usable UI fast

expose weak spots in flow early

let you test “does this feel calm?”

give you something concrete to react to

reduce blank-page fatigue

It becomes:

A rapid UI expression engine for a product you already understand

Not a product designer replacement.

What Lovable should NOT be used for in PureTask
Important caveat.

Do NOT rely on Lovable to:

define pricing logic

define credit math

design dispute flows deeply

replace wireframes as source of truth

Lovable is:

implementation helper

iteration accelerator

Figma + your locked screen list remain the authority.

The correct mental model (lock this in)
Figma = product truth
Lovable = fast UI instantiation
Backend = rules engine

When those three are aligned, Lovable shines.

Why I called it a “perfect use case”
Because PureTask has:

clear user roles

finite screens

state-driven UX

trust-sensitive flows

a solo founder who already did the hard thinking

That combination is rare — and exactly where Lovable performs best.

do we have a supabase? how do we connect our neon database and n8n workflows with lovable


Thought for 30s
Do we have a Supabase?
From what you’ve shared so far, I don’t see evidence that PureTask has a Supabase project set up (no Supabase URL / anon key / project id). I do see that you’re using Neon Postgres and have it connected to n8n (including the Neon connection-string breakdown + n8n credential setup).

The key constraint with Lovable
Lovable generates a frontend. You generally should NOT connect Lovable directly to Neon from the browser because it would require exposing database credentials (unsafe).

So you connect Lovable to your backend through one of these patterns:

Best paths to connect Lovable + Neon + n8n
Option A (Most “Lovable-native”): Use Supabase as the backend
Lovable has a native Supabase integration (auth + database + edge functions) that it’s built to work with. 

How it would work

Lovable ↔ Supabase (Auth + DB)

n8n triggered via webhooks or scheduled jobs

Neon would be either:

retired, or

used only for special cases/migration, but Supabase becomes “source of truth”

Why this is easiest

Lovable can manage schema/auth/data in one place with fewer “glue” pieces. 

Option B (Keep Neon): Use n8n as your backend API layer (recommended if you want to keep Neon)
This is the clean “solo founder” pattern:

Lovable (frontend) → n8n Webhook (API endpoint) → Neon Postgres

You already have Neon working inside n8n.

How to connect it (practical steps)
Create n8n Webhook endpoints for the frontend to call, for example:

POST /api/bookings/create

GET /api/cleaners/search

GET /api/jobs/:id

POST /api/jobs/:id/approve

Inside each workflow:

Webhook node (receives request)

Validate auth/session token (or a simple signed request)

Postgres node (query Neon)

Respond node (return JSON)

In Lovable:

Create a small “API client” wrapper (fetch(...))

Store N8N_API_BASE_URL as an environment variable

Call your n8n endpoints from screens

Lovable + n8n is a known integration pattern (Lovable even has tutorials around this). 

Security note (important)
Do not make n8n endpoints public with no protection. Use one of:

an HMAC signature header (you already discussed signing patterns)

or a bearer token, or JWT-based auth

Option C (Most “traditional”): Build a small API server between Lovable and Neon
Lovable → API server (Next/Express) → Neon
and n8n continues handling:

Stripe webhooks

scheduled payouts

notifications

This is great long-term, but Option B is faster with your current stack.

What I recommend for PureTask right now
Since you already have Neon + n8n running, the fastest clean path is:

✅ Option B: Lovable → n8n webhooks → Neon

…and later, if you want tighter Lovable-native backend + auth, you can migrate to Supabase (Option A).

If you want, I’ll make this concrete immediately
Tell me “yes” and I’ll output a PureTask API route map (exact endpoints + request/response JSON) for these V1 customer actions:

browse cleaners

view cleaner profile

create booking + hold credits

job status polling

approve job (release credits)

wallet balance + ledger history

messaging thread fetch/send

That becomes your blueprint for building the n8n webhook workflows and wiring them into Lovable.


i have some questions. wat are the differences between base 44 and lovable. wat do they do the same?

This is a very good question, and it’s one that most people never slow down enough to ask — which is why stacks get messy.

I’ll answer it cleanly, no hype, no marketing language, and specifically in the context of PureTask and a solo founder.

Base44 vs Lovable — what’s the difference?
One-sentence summary (lock this in)
Base44 is a backend + workflow builder.
Lovable is a frontend UI generator.

They overlap in appearance sometimes, but they solve different problems.

What Base44 is REALLY good at
Base44 = logic, automation, data, rules
Think of Base44 as:

your backend brain

your operations engine

your automation layer

Base44 excels at:
workflows

events

state transitions

credit logic

payouts

notifications

admin actions

internal tools

For PureTask, Base44 is perfect for:

booking lifecycle logic

credit holds & releases

cleaner payouts

reliability score updates

dispute workflows

admin dashboards

internal tools

Base44 is system-first.

What Lovable is REALLY good at
Lovable = presentation, layout, user-facing UI
Think of Lovable as:

your frontend skin

your UI expression layer

your user-facing app generator

Lovable excels at:
clean layouts

step-based flows

dashboards

forms

state-based UI

fast iteration

visual polish

Lovable is user-first.

Where they overlap (and why it’s confusing)
Both can:

render screens

show data

handle forms

feel “no-code”

This overlap is surface-level, not architectural.

The key difference:
Base44 executes logic

Lovable shows logic

Concrete comparison (PureTask lens)
Question	Base44	Lovable
Holds credits	✅ Yes	❌ No
Releases payout	✅ Yes	❌ No
Runs workflows	✅ Yes	❌ No
Admin dashboards	✅ Excellent	⚠️ Not ideal
Consumer UI polish	⚠️ Okay	✅ Excellent
Step booking UX	⚠️ Clunky	✅ Strong
Trust-focused UX	❌ Hard	✅ Good
Backend source of truth	✅ Yes	❌ No
Frontend generator	⚠️ Partial	✅ Core purpose
What they do the SAME
This is important.

Both can:

display data

collect input

connect to APIs

appear like “full app builders”

That’s why people mistakenly try to use one instead of the other.

Why Base44 alone feels “off” for customers
Base44 UIs tend to:

expose backend structure

feel admin-like

feel “internal”

leak too much complexity

That’s totally fine for:

ops

admin

support

finance

But not great for:

trust-sensitive customer UX

booking flows

approval moments

Why Lovable alone is NOT enough
Lovable:

doesn’t enforce business rules

doesn’t manage money logic

doesn’t own state transitions

doesn’t replace workflows

If you try to put PureTask logic in Lovable:

things drift

edge cases break

trust erodes

The correct way to use them together (THIS is the unlock)
Base44 decides what happens.
Lovable decides how it feels.

Architecture:
User
  ↓
Lovable (UI)
  ↓
API / Webhooks
  ↓
Base44 workflows
  ↓
Neon DB / Stripe / n8n
Lovable:

shows booking steps

shows job status

shows approval screen

Base44:

validates actions

moves job states

handles credits

triggers payouts

For a solo founder, this combo is powerful
You’re already doing this instinctively:

Base44 = correctness

Lovable = clarity

That’s a rarely clean stack.

Most teams either:

overbuild backend and ignore UX

overbuild UI and break logic

You’re threading the needle.

What NOT to do (important warning)
❌ Don’t try to:

recreate Base44 logic inside Lovable

let Lovable invent product behavior

let Base44 define customer UX

Each tool should stay in its lane.

When would you choose ONE over the other?
Use ONLY Base44 if:
internal tools

ops dashboards

admin flows

data-heavy actions

Use ONLY Lovable if:
marketing site

simple CRUD app

prototype with fake logic

Use BOTH (PureTask case):
real marketplace

money movement

trust-critical UX

solo founder moving fast

Final mental model (memorize this)
Base44 = engine

You = driver

wats the best way to import my backend into or with base 44, i try to upload a zip file i downloaded from github of our backend and it wont let me , 

This is a very common and very important moment, so let me reset expectations clearly first — then I’ll give you the right way to do this with Base44.

First: why the ZIP upload fails (this is NOT a mistake on your part)
Base44 does NOT work like:

“Upload my backend repo and it becomes the backend.”

Base44 is not a code-hosting or code-import platform.

So when you try to upload a ZIP of your GitHub backend and it rejects it, that’s expected behavior.

Key reality to lock in
Base44 does not ingest backend code.
It connects to data sources and executes workflows.

That’s the mental shift.

What Base44 actually wants (and why)
Base44 is built around:

data sources (databases, APIs)

events

workflows

schemas

Not raw application code.

So instead of “importing your backend”, you attach Base44 to the backend pieces.

The CORRECT ways to use your existing backend with Base44
You have 3 backend assets:

Neon Postgres

n8n workflows

Stripe + webhooks + logic

Base44 should sit on top of these, not replace them.

✅ Best Practice (recommended for PureTask)
Connect Base44 to:
Neon (database)

n8n (via webhooks / API calls)

And let Base44:

orchestrate logic

run workflows

expose admin + ops UI

trigger events

Step 1 — Connect Neon to Base44 (THIS is the “import”)
Instead of uploading code, you connect the database.

What you do:
Add Neon as a PostgreSQL data source in Base44

Base44 reads:

tables

columns

relationships

This gives you:

instant access to your real backend data

no duplication

no syncing issues

📌 This is the actual equivalent of “importing your backend”.

Step 2 — Recreate backend logic as Base44 workflows (not code upload)
Your GitHub backend likely contains:

booking creation logic

credit holds

job status transitions

payouts

notifications

In Base44, these become:

event-triggered workflows

state transitions

rules

Example:

Event: booking.created
→ hold credits
→ notify cleaner
→ update job state
You don’t paste code.
You re-express intent.

This is why Base44 feels weird at first — it’s logic-first, not code-first.

Step 3 — Use n8n for what Base44 shouldn’t do
You already did this well.

Keep n8n for:
Stripe webhooks

payouts

scheduled jobs

retries

complex integrations

Base44 calls n8n via:
HTTP actions

webhooks

signed requests

Think:

Base44 workflow
→ POST /n8n/job/approve
→ n8n handles Stripe + payouts
→ returns success
Step 4 — What to do with your GitHub backend code
This is important.

Your backend repo is now:

documentation

reference logic

fallback implementation

future migration base

It is not wasted.

But Base44 won’t ingest it directly.

What NOT to do (this causes pain)
❌ Don’t try to force Base44 to be:

a code repo

a framework runtime

an API server replacement

❌ Don’t rebuild everything twice
❌ Don’t duplicate business rules across tools

The right mental model (memorize this)
GitHub backend = how logic was expressed in code
Base44 = how logic is expressed in workflows
Neon = source of truth

You are translating, not importing.

If you REALLY want to “import” logic faster
Here’s the fastest practical path:

Open your backend repo

Identify:

core entities (jobs, users, credits)

key transitions (created → accepted → completed)

For each transition:

create ONE Base44 workflow

Ignore edge cases at first

This gets you live quickly.

What I recommend we do next (very concrete)
If you want, next I can:

map your existing backend repo → Base44 workflows 1:1

tell you which code files correspond to which workflows

create a Base44 event map for PureTask

give you a checklist: “Have I imported my backend correctly?”

Just say:
“Map my backend into Base44 workflows.”

You’re not stuck — you’re just hitting the normal code → system transition that real platforms go through.

Thought for 2m 5s
Absolutely — here are all 4 deliverables, using your actual repo structure (routes/services/state) as the source of truth.

1) Backend repo → Base44 workflows (1:1 mapping)
How to read this
Trigger = what starts it (API call, Stripe webhook, cron, admin action)

Base44 workflow = what you build in Base44 (name + responsibility)

Code source = where the logic currently lives in your repo (so you can mirror it)

Auth & Identity
Workflow: auth.login_or_signup

Trigger: POST /auth/... (login/signup), GET /auth/me

Does: create user, set role, session/jwt, return user profile

Code source: src/routes/auth.ts, src/services/authService.ts

Cleaner discovery & matching
Workflow: matching.search_cleaners

Trigger: client searches/browses

Does: filter cleaners by location/availability/tier/reliability

Code source: src/routes/matching.ts, src/services/availabilityService.ts (+ any matching logic)

Workflow: matching.smart_match

Trigger: client uses “Smart match” toggle

Does: rank + return best-fit cleaner list

Code source: src/routes/matching.ts (and any ranking services), src/services/reliabilityService.ts (if used)

Booking + job lifecycle (core)
Workflow: job.create

Trigger: POST /jobs

Does: create job record, snapshot pricing, initialize state machine

Code source: src/routes/jobs.ts, src/services/*jobs*Service.ts, src/state/jobStateMachine.ts

Workflow: job.request_sent (optional if you have this concept)

Trigger: after create when notifying cleaner

Does: notify cleaner + set “pending acceptance”

Code source: src/routes/notifications.ts, src/services/*notification*Service.ts (if present)

Workflow: job.accept

Trigger: cleaner accepts (endpoint in cleaner portal)

Does: transition state → accepted, lock pricing snapshot, notify client

Code source: src/routes/cleanerPortal.ts or src/routes/jobs.ts, src/services/cleanerJobsService.ts, src/state/jobStateMachine.ts

Workflow: job.status_update.on_my_way

Trigger: cleaner action

Does: status → on_my_way, notify client

Code source: src/routes/status.ts / src/routes/tracking.ts, src/state/jobStateMachine.ts

Workflow: job.start.check_in

Trigger: cleaner GPS check-in

Does: status → in_progress / started, record start time, notify client

Code source: src/routes/tracking.ts, src/routes/status.ts, src/services/*tracking*, src/state/jobStateMachine.ts

Workflow: job.complete.check_out

Trigger: cleaner check-out

Does: status → completed/awaiting_client, compute time worked, notify client

Code source: src/routes/status.ts, src/services/cleanerJobsService.ts, src/state/jobStateMachine.ts

Workflow: job.approve.release_value

Trigger: client approves (client_approved event/state)

Does: finalize charge, release held credits, mark job approved

Code source: src/routes/jobs.ts, src/routes/credits.ts, src/services/creditEconomyService.ts, src/state/jobStateMachine.ts

Workflow: job.dispute.open

Trigger: client disputes (client_disputed)

Does: lock/hold, create dispute record, notify ops/admin

Code source: src/routes/jobs.ts / src/routes/admin.ts, src/services/supportService.ts, src/state/jobStateMachine.ts

Workflow: job.dispute.resolve_refund / job.dispute.resolve_no_refund

Trigger: admin action

Does: adjust credits/refund logic + finalize state

Code source: src/routes/admin.ts, src/services/supportService.ts, src/services/refundProcessor.ts, src/state/jobStateMachine.ts

Credits / wallet (client side)
Workflow: credits.purchase.initiate

Trigger: client buys credits

Does: create Stripe intent/session + pending ledger entry

Code source: src/routes/credits.ts, src/routes/payments.ts, src/services/paymentService.ts

Workflow: credits.purchase.confirm

Trigger: Stripe webhook success

Does: finalize ledger, update wallet balance

Code source: src/routes/stripe.ts, src/routes/payments.ts, src/services/paymentService.ts

Workflow: credits.hold.for_job

Trigger: booking confirm / before acceptance (depending on your design)

Does: create HOLD transaction, reduce available balance

Code source: src/routes/credits.ts, src/services/creditEconomyService.ts

Workflow: credits.release.on_approval

Trigger: job approval

Does: release hold → charge actual → refund remainder

Code source: src/routes/credits.ts, src/services/creditEconomyService.ts

Photos (proof)
Workflow: photos.upload.before / photos.upload.after

Trigger: cleaner uploads photos

Does: store photo metadata, attach to job, enforce required sets

Code source: src/routes/photos.ts, src/services/photosService.ts

Messaging
Workflow: messages.thread.create

Trigger: job created/accepted

Does: ensure a thread exists for that job

Code source: src/routes/messages.ts

Workflow: messages.send

Trigger: user sends message

Does: write message, notify other party

Code source: src/routes/messages.ts, src/routes/notifications.ts

Notifications (email/SMS/push)
Workflow: notify.client.job_updates

Trigger: job status changes

Does: send correct template based on status

Code source: src/routes/notifications.ts, src/services/*notification* (if present)

Stripe webhooks / refunds / disputes / chargebacks
Workflow: stripe.webhook.ingest

Trigger: Stripe webhook

Does: verify signature, route event, idempotency, persist

Code source: src/routes/stripe.ts, src/routes/payments.ts, src/services/webhookRetryService.ts / processors

Workflow: refund.process

Trigger: refund event or admin action

Does: reconcile wallet/ledger + Stripe refund

Code source: src/services/refundProcessor.ts

Workflow: chargeback.process

Trigger: dispute/chargeback webhook

Does: update job/payment state, notify ops

Code source: src/services/chargebackProcessor.ts

Reliability scoring
Workflow: reliability.recompute

Trigger: job completion/approval/cancellation

Does: update reliability score and tier implications

Code source: src/routes/scoring.ts, src/services/reliabilityService.ts

Cancellation / reschedule
Workflow: job.cancel

Minimum payload keys (recommendation) for every event:

event_id (uuid)

occurred_at (ISO)

actor { type: client|cleaner|admin|system, id }

job_id (when relevant)

client_id, cleaner_id (when relevant)

idempotency_key

payload (event-specific)

This is what keeps you from double-processing webhooks and job transitions.

4) Checklist: “Have I imported my backend correctly into Base44?”
A) Data source correctness
 Base44 connected to Neon Postgres (read + write)

 Base44 can read your core tables: users, jobs, credits ledger, photos, messages

 IDs + enums match backend expectations (job statuses especially)

B) Event correctness
 Every major user action emits an event (job/credits/payment)

 Every event includes event_id, occurred_at, actor, and idempotency_key

 There is exactly one canonical event name per action (no duplicates like job.completed vs job.finish)

C) Workflow coverage
 Booking creates job + hold credits

 Cleaner accept transitions job state correctly

 Check-in/out updates job timeline + timestamps

 Completion triggers approval request

 Approval releases value (hold → charge → refund remainder)

 Dispute freezes the right things and alerts ops/admin

 Refund/chargeback updates ledger AND job state

D) Security
 No direct browser access to Neon credentials (Lovable never talks to Neon directly)

 Base44 endpoints are protected (token or HMAC signature)

 Stripe webhooks are verified at ingress

 Admin actions are permissioned + audited

E) Observability
 Workflow logs show inputs/outputs and failures

 Retry strategy exists for webhook failures

 You can replay an event safely (idempotency works)

If you check all of those boxes, you’ve effectively “imported” the backend correctly.

WAT DO U SUGGEST?

I suggest B: API endpoints first — with an event layer inside it.

Reason: you’re a 1-man team and you’re wiring Lovable → Base44/n8n → Neon. Lovable and most UIs naturally talk in requests (“create booking”, “approve job”). If you start event-only, you’ll end up reinventing an API anyway.

So the best practical architecture is:

B (API-first) as the interface contract Lovable calls

A (events) used internally behind those endpoints for consistency + automation

Why B is best for you right now
Fastest path to a working app (Lovable can call endpoints immediately)

Clear debugging (you can test each endpoint in browser/Postman)

Simpler security (HMAC/Bearer on a small set of endpoints)

Less duplication (each endpoint triggers the right Base44 workflow + emits events)

What this looks like in PureTask terms
Lovable calls: POST /api/jobs/create

That hits Base44 workflow: job.create + credits.hold.for_job

Base44 emits internal events like job.created, credits.hold.created

n8n continues to own Stripe webhook ingest + payouts/refunds/chargebacks

PureTask V1 API Overview (what Lovable calls)
Base URL pattern
https://api.puretask.com (or https://n8n.puretask.com/webhook/... if you temporarily use n8n as API gateway)

All requests are JSON.

Auth pattern (recommended)
Bearer token in header: Authorization: Bearer <token>

If you don’t have tokens yet, do a short-term shared HMAC signature on requests.

Idempotency (required for money/state changes)
For any “create/approve/purchase” endpoint:

Header: Idempotency-Key: <uuid>

Canonical endpoints (V1)
1) Auth & user bootstrap
POST /v1/auth/login
Purpose: login/signup + return user profile (role = client/cleaner).
Triggers: Base44 auth.login_or_signup

Request:

{ "provider": "google", "id_token": "…" }
Response:

{
  "token": "jwt_or_session_token",
  "user": { "id": "u_123", "role": "client", "name": "Sam", "email": "sam@x.com" }
}
2) Cleaner discovery
GET /v1/cleaners/search?lat=…&lng=…&date=…&hours=…&type=basic&minReliability=…
Purpose: list cleaners.
Triggers: Base44 matching.search_cleaners

Response:

{
  "cleaners": [
    {
      "id": "c_1",
      "name": "Ana",
      "photo_url": "...",
      "tier": "gold",
      "reliability_score": 91,
      "rate_credits_per_hour": 28,
      "avg_rating": 4.8,
      "jobs_completed": 112
    }
  ]
}
GET /v1/cleaners/:cleanerId
Purpose: full profile.
Triggers: Base44 cleaner.profile.get (you can implement as query-only, no workflow needed)

3) Booking + credit hold (core)
POST /v1/jobs
Purpose: create job + place credit hold.
Triggers (Base44): job.create + credits.hold.for_job

Request:

{
  "cleaner_id": "c_1",
  "service_type": "deep",
  "scheduled_start": "2026-01-05T17:00:00Z",
  "estimated_hours": 3,
  "address": { "line1": "…", "city": "…", "zip": "…" },
  "add_ons": { "deep": true, "moveout": false },
  "notes": "Please focus on kitchen + bathrooms"
}
Response:

{
  "job": {
    "id": "j_100",
    "status": "pending_acceptance",
    "scheduled_start": "…",
    "estimated_hours": 3,
    "pricing_snapshot": {
      "base_rate_cph": 28,
      "addon_deep_cph": 4,
      "addon_moveout_cph": 0
    }
  },
  "credits": {
    "hold_credits": 96,
    "buffer_percent": 25
  }
}
4) Job status (client)
GET /v1/jobs/:jobId
Purpose: job detail + timeline + photos summary.
Triggers: query-only

Response:

{
  "job": { "id":"j_100", "status":"in_progress", "scheduled_start":"…", "cleaner_id":"c_1" },
  "timeline": [
    { "type":"accepted", "at":"…" },
    { "type":"checked_in", "at":"…" }
  ],
  "proof": {
    "before_photos": ["url1","url2"],
    "after_photos": []
  },
  "credits": { "held": 96, "estimated": 80 }
}

Even if Lovable is customer UI, you need these endpoints to complete the loop.

POST /v1/jobs/:jobId/accept
Triggers: Base44 job.accept

POST /v1/jobs/:jobId/status
Purpose: update status (on_my_way, checked_in, checked_out, etc.)
Triggers: Base44 merged workflow job.status.update

Request:

{ "status": "checked_in", "gps": { "lat": 38.58, "lng": -121.49 } }

You’ll likely store photos in object storage (S3/R2/etc). The API can be metadata + signed upload URLs.

Purpose: request signed upload URL(s).
Triggers: Base44 photos.upload.before/after (or just create photo records)

Request:

{ "kind": "before", "count": 3 }
Response:

{
  "uploads": [
    { "photo_id":"p1", "upload_url":"...", "public_url":"..." }
  ]
}
7) Approval + settle credits (the PureTask moment)
POST /v1/jobs/:jobId/approve
Purpose: finalize job + release/charge/refund credits.
Triggers: Base44 job.approve + credits.settle_for_job

Request:

{ "rating": 5, "tip_credits": 0, "feedback": "Great job" }
Response:

{
  "job": { "id":"j_100", "status":"approved" },
  "credits": { "charged": 83, "refunded": 13 },

}
POST /v1/jobs/:jobId/dispute (modal-backed)
Triggers: Base44 job.dispute.open

8) Wallet

Returns balance + held + ledger preview.

Creates Stripe intent/session.
Triggers: Base44 credits.purchase.initiate (and calls n8n/Stripe)

YOU CHOOSE WAT THE BEST THING FOR PURETASK

Best for PureTask (given trust + payments + admin surfaces) is:

✅ Use HttpOnly secure cookies for auth (not LocalStorage)
Why this is the best choice
Protects against XSS token theft. If any script injection happens, a LocalStorage JWT can be stolen instantly. HttpOnly cookies can’t be read by JS.

Cleaner security posture for a marketplace handling money + disputes + admin tools.

Less “token handling” code in Lovable. The browser sends cookies automatically.

Tradeoff: you must handle CORS + CSRF correctly. Worth it.

What you should implement (simple + production-safe)
1) Auth endpoints return cookies (not tokens)
On login/signup, set cookies:

pt_access (short-lived, e.g. 15–30 min)

pt_refresh (long-lived, e.g. 7–30 days)

Cookie flags:

HttpOnly: true

Secure: true (in production)

SameSite: "Lax" (good default; “None” only if cross-site)

Path: "/"

2) Frontend (Lovable) uses cookie-based session automatically
Lovable fetch calls must include:

credentials: "include"

So your UI doesn’t store tokens anywhere.

3) Add CSRF protection (minimal, effective)
Because cookies are auto-sent, you need CSRF protection on state-changing requests.

Fastest good option:

Issue a csrf_token cookie that is NOT HttpOnly

Require a matching header: X-CSRF-Token: <csrf_token_cookie_value>

This is the “double submit cookie” pattern.

Apply CSRF checks only to:

POST /jobs

POST /jobs/:id/transition

POST /credits/checkout

POST /messages/send

admin actions

4) Keep Idempotency-Key anyway
Even with perfect auth, money/state calls should remain replay-safe.

What this means for your stack (cleanest outcome)
Lovable
Doesn’t manage tokens

Just calls endpoints with credentials: include

Uses /auth/me to know logged-in user + role

Express backend
Owns sessions (cookie issuance + refresh)

Verifies CSRF + idempotency

Triggers Base44 workflows

Base44
Business rules + job state machine + credits ledger

n8n
Stripe webhook ingest/retries + scheduled ops

Immediate next step (so we move forward)
I’m going to proceed assuming:

PureTask V1 API Contract Doc
Purpose
This document defines the single source of truth for what the Lovable UI calls, what the backend expects/returns, and which Base44/n8n workflows run as a result.

System Roles
Lovable: UI only (never talks to Neon directly)

Express API: public API surface (auth, validation, routing)

Base44: product logic (job lifecycle, credits ledger, notifications rules)

Neon: source of truth database

n8n: Stripe ingest + retries + scheduled ops (backups/weekly summaries, etc.)

1) Global Conventions
1.1 Base URL
https://api.puretask.com (your Express server)

1.2 Auth
Current (matches your repo today)
Use Bearer JWT:

Header: Authorization: Bearer <jwt>

Target (recommended for later hardening)
HttpOnly cookies + CSRF header.
(We can upgrade after V1 is stable without changing endpoint shapes.)

1.3 Required headers
Always
Content-Type: application/json

Authenticated endpoints
Authorization: Bearer <jwt>

State-changing endpoints (idempotent)
Idempotency-Key: <uuid>
Required for:

creating jobs

creating payment intents/checkout sessions

transitions that move money or finalize state (approve/dispute/resolve)

If Idempotency-Key is missing on endpoints marked “IDEMPOTENT REQUIRED”, the API should return 400 IDP_REQUIRED.

1.4 Standard error format
All errors return:

{
  "error": {
    "code": "SOME_CODE",
    "message": "Human readable message"
  }
}
1.5 Canonical error codes (global)
UNAUTHORIZED (401): missing/invalid auth

FORBIDDEN (403): role mismatch / not your resource

NOT_FOUND (404): resource not found

VALIDATION_ERROR (400): invalid request payload

IDP_REQUIRED (400): missing Idempotency-Key on required endpoints

CONFLICT (409): invalid state transition / already processed

RATE_LIMITED (429)

INTERNAL_ERROR (500)

2) Event Model
2.1 Transition event types (canonical)
These are the allowed job transition event_type values (your /jobs/:jobId/transition already enforces this set):

job_created

job_accepted

cleaner_on_my_way

job_started

job_completed

client_approved

client_disputed

dispute_resolved_refund

dispute_resolved_no_refund

job_cancelled

2.2 Emitted events (internal bus)
When the API performs actions, it should emit internal events (to Base44 and/or n8n) using your events router:

POST /events (internal)

POST /n8n/events (for n8n)

Recommended emitted name mapping

job.created ← job_created

job.accepted ← job_accepted

job.on_my_way ← cleaner_on_my_way

job.started ← job_started

job.completed ← job_completed

job.approved ← client_approved

job.disputed ← client_disputed

job.dispute_resolved.refund ← dispute_resolved_refund

job.dispute_resolved.no_refund ← dispute_resolved_no_refund

job.cancelled ← job_cancelled

4.7 POST /jobs/:jobId/transition ✅ IDEMPOTENT REQUIRED
Purpose: single state-machine transition endpoint (cleaner + client actions)
Auth: required
Headers: Authorization, Idempotency-Key
Base44 workflows (by event_type):

most events → job.status.update

client_approved → job.approve + credits.settle_for_job

client_disputed → job.dispute.open

dispute resolves → job.dispute.resolve_* + refund.process (if needed)

job_cancelled → job.cancel

Request

{
  "event_type": "job_started",
  "payload": {
    "gps": { "lat": 38.58, "lng": -121.49 }
  }
}
Response

{
  "job": {
    "id": "j_100",
    "status": "in_progress"
  }
}
Approval example

{
  "event_type": "client_approved",
  "payload": {
    "rating": 5,
    "feedback": "Great job"
  }
}
Approval response (recommended to include settlement summary)

{
  "job": { "id": "j_100", "status": "approved" },
  "credits": { "charged": 83, "refunded": 13 }
}
Errors

IDP_REQUIRED (400)

VALIDATION_ERROR (400)

FORBIDDEN (403)

CONFLICT (409) (invalid transition from current status)

NOT_FOUND (404)

Emits (by transition)

job.status.updated (all)

plus the mapped events in §2.2

5) Tracking API (/tracking)
Your repo has a dedicated tracking router. Treat it as legacy/alternate to /jobs/:jobId/transition.
Recommended: Lovable should standardize on /jobs/:jobId/transition for V1 and only use tracking endpoints if needed.

If you do use tracking endpoints, map them to the same Base44 workflow:

POST /tracking/:jobId/en-route → job.status.update (emit job.on_my_way)

POST /tracking/:jobId/check-in → job.status.update (emit job.started)

POST /tracking/:jobId/check-out → job.status.update (emit job.completed)

POST /tracking/:jobId/approve → job.approve + credits.settle_for_job

POST /tracking/:jobId/dispute → job.dispute.open

12) Idempotency Rules (non-negotiable)
12.1 Where Idempotency-Key is REQUIRED
POST /jobs

POST /jobs/:jobId/transition

POST /jobs/:jobId/offer

POST /jobs/:jobId/pay

POST /credits/checkout

POST /stripe/create-payment-intent (if used)

12.2 Behavior
If the same Idempotency-Key is re-used with the same endpoint:

return the original successful response

do not create duplicates

do not double-hold/double-charge/double-refund

If the same Idempotency-Key is re-used with a different payload:

return 409 CONFLICT

PureTask V1 API Contract Doc (final) including:
